Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) tend to result in a high yield. This is likely due to the ability of these groups to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group (e.g., benzoic acid derivatives) tend to result in a high yield if they also contain additional functional groups like amino or hydroxy groups. This suggests that additional functional groups may enhance the interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) tend to result in a low yield unless they are part of a larger aromatic system. This could be due to the potential for sulfanyl groups to poison the catalyst. Examples of low yield include 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid and 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (e.g., methoxyethoxy) tend to result in a low yield. This may be due to steric hindrance or poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. This could be due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the formyl group, which may destabilize the interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine may alter the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring tend to result in a high yield, likely due to the nitrogen atom's ability to coordinate with metal centers. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield, possibly due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

**End of Rules**

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current validation accuracy of 0.6286 is significantly lower than the previous reference accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be as effective in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy indicates potential underfitting, where the model is not capturing the complexity of the data. This could be due to overly simplistic rules or a lack of rules that address key features of the data.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 consistently shows a negative SHAP value, indicating it may be underestimating the loading. Rule 7 also shows negative SHAP values in these cases, suggesting it might not be capturing the positive influence of pyridine rings effectively.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is high and the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the impact of carboxylic acid and amino groups.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement in the rules. The lower validation accuracy compared to previous iterations indicates that the current set of rules may not be capturing the necessary features to predict the loading accurately.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - The SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 1 and Rule 7 are frequently involved in discrepancies between predicted and experimental values. This suggests that these rules may need refinement to better capture the nuances of the data.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rule 1:**
   - Given its frequent involvement in discrepancies, Rule 1 should be refined to better account for the conditions under which carboxylic acid and amino groups lead to high loading. Consider incorporating additional context or conditions that might influence their effectiveness.

2. **Adjust Rule 7:**
   - Rule 7, which deals with pyridine rings, should be adjusted to better capture their impact. This could involve considering additional structural features or interactions that enhance the loading.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential underfitting, consider expanding the rule set to include additional functional groups or structural motifs that may influence loading. This could help capture more of the complexity in the data.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use the insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules with high positive SHAP values for overestimated loadings and high negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings. This iterative process can help improve the model's ability to generalize across different molecules.

5. **Incorporate Additional Features:**
   - Consider incorporating additional features or descriptors that may influence loading, such as electronic properties, steric effects, or solvent interactions. This could provide a more comprehensive view of the factors influencing loading and improve the model's predictive power.

The coordination ability of these groups with metal centers is enhanced when they are spatially close, improving catalytic activity. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield. These groups enhance the interaction with the catalyst by increasing electron density. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. The potential for sulfanyl groups to poison the catalyst is reduced when they are part of a larger aromatic system. Examples of low yield include 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid and 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the formyl group destabilizes the interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents tend to result in a high yield. - **Rule 8**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

4. **Train Leverage**: The leverage has slightly decreased from 0.0177 to 0.0168. While leverage is positive, indicating a stronger association than expected by chance, the slight decrease suggests that the improvement in confidence and lift might be coming at the cost of slightly less coverage of the dataset.

**Recommendations for Improvement**:

- **Focus on Increasing Support**: While the current rules are more confident and have a higher lift, exploring ways to increase support without sacrificing these metrics could make the rules applicable to a broader dataset. This might involve identifying additional conditions or modifiers that align with the current high-confidence rules.

- **Refine Rule Conditions**: Consider refining the conditions of the rules to capture more instances without losing specificity. This could involve exploring combinations of existing conditions or introducing new chemical groups that align with the high-yield or low-yield outcomes.

- **Balance Between Specificity and Generality**: Strive for a balance between specificity (high confidence and lift) and generality (higher support). This might involve iterative testing and validation with different subsets of the data to ensure the rules are robust across various scenarios.

- **Continuous Monitoring**: Keep monitoring the metrics over subsequent iterations to ensure that improvements in one area do not lead to significant declines in others. The goal should be to maintain or improve confidence and lift while gradually increasing support.

Overall, the current rules are performing well in terms of confidence and lift, indicating that they are effective at identifying meaningful patterns. The focus should now be on expanding the applicability of these rules while maintaining their accuracy.

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some improvement in validation accuracy from 0.628 to 0.657, indicating that the rules are becoming more effective. However, the accuracy is still below the previous reference accuracies of 0.76, suggesting that there is room for further improvement.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The consistent improvement in validation accuracy suggests that the model is not overfitting. However, the lower accuracy compared to previous reference values indicates potential underfitting, where the model might not be capturing all the necessary patterns in the data.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 and Rule 2 often have high positive SHAP values, indicating these rules might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 4 and Rule 6 often have high negative SHAP values, suggesting these rules might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The increase in validation accuracy from 0.628 to 0.657 suggests that the current rules are moving in the right direction. However, the gap between the current and reference accuracies indicates that further refinement is needed.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 1 and Rule 2 are contributing significantly to high loading predictions, which might be leading to overestimations. Adjusting these rules could help in reducing false positives.
   - Rules 4 and 6, which have high negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings, might need to be adjusted to better capture the conditions leading to high experimental loadings.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1:** Consider adding conditions to limit the influence of carboxylic acid and amino groups in specific contexts, such as when they are part of larger, less reactive structures.
   - **Rule 2:** Re-evaluate the impact of electron-donating groups in phenyl groups, possibly adding constraints based on the overall molecular structure.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 4:** Reassess the impact of formyl groups on aromatic rings, considering additional factors that might mitigate their electron-withdrawing effects.
   - **Rule 6:** Explore the role of pyridine rings with electron-donating substituents in more detail, potentially incorporating additional structural features that enhance their interaction with catalysts.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more diverse training data to capture a wider range of molecular interactions and reduce underfitting.
   - Consider using ensemble methods or hybrid models that combine rule-based and data-driven approaches to improve generalization.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively test and refine rules, focusing on those with the most significant impact on predictions.
   - Regularly evaluate the model's performance on both training and validation sets to ensure that improvements are consistent and sustainable.

By implementing these recommendations, the model's accuracy and generalization should improve, bringing it closer to the reference accuracies and enhancing its predictive capabilities.

These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. The potential for sulfanyl groups to poison the catalyst is reduced when they are part of a larger aromatic system. Examples of low yield include 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid and 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the formyl group destabilizes the interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. The potential for sulfanyl groups to poison the catalyst is reduced when they are part of a larger aromatic system. Examples of low yield include 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid and 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

3. **Train Lift**: The current train lift is 1.637, which is lower than the previous iteration's values of 1.751 and 1.809. Lift is a crucial metric as it indicates the strength of the association between the rule conditions and the outcomes. A lift greater than 1 suggests that the rule is useful, but the decrease in lift indicates that the current rules may not be as impactful as before.

4. **Train Leverage**: The current train leverage is 0.013, which is slightly lower than the previous iteration's values of 0.018 and 0.017. Leverage measures the additional support the rule receives over what would be expected by chance. The positive leverage indicates a stronger association than expected by chance, but the decrease suggests a slight weakening in the rule's effectiveness.

**Comments and Suggestions for Improvement:**

- **Focus on Lift**: Since lift is a critical measure of the rule's effectiveness, efforts should be made to increase it. This could involve refining the rules to better capture the underlying patterns in the data or exploring additional features that might enhance the rule's predictive power.

- **Broaden Rule Applicability**: The decrease in support suggests that the rules might be too specific. Consider generalizing some of the conditions to increase the applicability of the rules without significantly compromising confidence and lift.

- **Evaluate Rule Conditions**: Reassess the conditions of the current rules to ensure they are capturing the most relevant features. It might be beneficial to explore interactions between different chemical groups or structural features that could lead to higher yields.

- **Iterate and Test**: Continue iterating on the rules, testing them against new data, and comparing the metrics to ensure that improvements are being made. It may also be helpful to use cross-validation to ensure that the rules generalize well to unseen data.

Overall, while the current rules show promise, there is room for improvement, particularly in enhancing the lift and ensuring the rules are not overly specific. By focusing on these areas, the rules can be made more robust and effective in predicting high-yield outcomes.

Final Metric Message:

    Target Name: yield
    GPT Model: gpt-4o-2024-08-06
    ML Model: ETC
    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. The potential for sulfanyl groups to poison the catalyst is reduced when they are part of a larger aromatic system. Examples of low yield include 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid and 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. The potential for sulfanyl groups to poison the catalyst is reduced when they are part of a larger aromatic system. Examples of low yield include 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid and 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

**End of Rules**

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

**End of Rules**

While this might suggest that the rules are more specific, it also means that they might not be as broadly applicable. However, the high confidence and lift values suggest that when the rules do apply, they are quite effective.

**Recommendations for Improvement**:

- **Broaden Applicability**: Consider revising or expanding the rules to increase their support. This could involve identifying additional conditions or modifying existing ones to capture a larger portion of the dataset without sacrificing too much confidence or lift.

- **Refine Specificity**: While high confidence and lift are desirable, ensure that the rules are not overly specific to the training data. This can be achieved by validating the rules on a separate test set to ensure they generalize well.

- **Explore Additional Features**: Investigate other potential features or conditions that could be incorporated into the rules to enhance their predictive power and applicability.

- **Iterative Testing**: Continue iterating on the rules, using the current metrics as a benchmark. Aim to maintain or improve confidence and lift while increasing support, ensuring that the rules remain robust and generalizable.

Overall, the current rules demonstrate strong predictive capabilities, but there is room to improve their applicability and ensure they are not overfitted to the training data.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some improvement in validation accuracy, increasing from 0.6286 to 0.6857. This suggests that the rules are becoming more effective in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.
   - However, the accuracy is still not optimal, indicating that there is room for further refinement of the rules.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The consistent improvement in validation accuracy without a significant drop suggests that the model is not overfitting. However, the relatively low accuracy indicates potential underfitting, where the model might not be capturing all the relevant features or interactions.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 2 and Rule 4 often have high negative SHAP values, suggesting they might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements Interpretation:**
   - The gradual improvement in accuracy suggests that the iterative refinement of rules is moving in the right direction. However, the current accuracy levels indicate that further iterations and refinements are necessary to reach a more optimal solution.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights specific rules that contribute significantly to the prediction errors. For instance, Rule 1 and Rule 2 frequently appear in cases of misprediction, indicating they might need adjustments.
   - The analysis also suggests that some rules might be too general or too specific, leading to incorrect predictions.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1**: Consider adding more specific conditions or exceptions to Rule 1 to prevent overestimation of loading. For example, refine the proximity or context in which carboxylic acid and amino groups lead to high yield.
   - **Rule 2**: Evaluate the impact of additional electron-withdrawing groups more carefully, as they might be causing underestimation in some cases.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 4**: Reassess the conditions under which formyl groups lead to low yield. Consider the influence of other substituents that might mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects.
   - **Rule 6**: Ensure that the electron-donating substituents on pyridine rings are adequately captured, as they might be underestimated in some contexts.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more nuanced rules that capture interactions between different functional groups, rather than treating them in isolation.
   - Consider incorporating additional features or rules that account for steric effects, which might be influencing the loading predictions.

4. **Iterative Refinement Using SHAP Insights:**
   - Use SHAP analysis to identify patterns in mispredictions and iteratively refine the rules. For example, if certain functional groups consistently lead to errors, investigate their interactions with other groups or the catalyst.
   - Regularly evaluate the impact of rule changes on validation accuracy to ensure that refinements are leading to genuine improvements.

By focusing on these areas, the rules can be refined to better capture the complex interactions that determine the loading of modifiers on catalysts, ultimately leading to improved prediction accuracy.

Final Metric Message:

    Target Name: yield
    GPT Model: gpt-4o-2024-08-06
    ML Model: ETC
    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. The potential for sulfanyl groups to poison the catalyst is reduced when they are part of a larger aromatic system. Examples of low yield include 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid and 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. The potential for sulfanyl groups to poison the catalyst is reduced when they are part of a larger aromatic system. Examples of low yield include 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid and 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. However, exceptions exist, such as 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, which shows a high yield. This suggests that other structural features or environmental factors may mitigate the sulfanyl group's negative effects.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some improvement in validation accuracy, reaching 0.6857, which is higher than some previous iterations. This suggests that the rules are becoming more effective in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The consistent improvement in validation accuracy without a significant drop in performance suggests that the model is not overfitting. However, the test accuracy is not provided for the current iteration, which makes it difficult to fully assess overfitting or underfitting.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 and Rule 4 often have high positive SHAP values, indicating potential overestimation issues.
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 6 and Rule 7 often have high negative SHAP values, indicating potential underestimation issues.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The increase in validation accuracy suggests that the rules are improving in capturing the correct patterns. However, without test accuracy, it's hard to determine if these improvements generalize well to unseen data.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that Rule 1 and Rule 4 might be contributing to overestimated loadings, while Rule 6 and Rule 7 might be contributing to underestimated loadings. This suggests a need to refine these rules to better align predictions with experimental values.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1:** Consider adding conditions to account for potential steric hindrance or electronic effects that might reduce the effectiveness of the carboxylic acid and amino group interaction.
   - **Rule 4:** Re-evaluate the impact of electron-donating groups on aromatic systems and consider additional structural features that might mitigate the formyl group's electron-withdrawing effects.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 6:** Investigate the specific substituents on the pyridine ring that might be leading to underestimation and refine the rule to better capture these effects.
   - **Rule 7:** Consider the context in which indole groups are present and whether additional structural features might enhance or reduce their effectiveness.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more diverse training data to ensure the rules are not overly tailored to the current dataset.
   - Consider using cross-validation techniques to better assess the model's ability to generalize.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively test and refine rules, focusing on those with the most significant impact on prediction discrepancies.
   - Continuously evaluate the impact of rule changes on both validation and test accuracy to ensure improvements are not just local optimizations.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve both the accuracy and generalization of the model, leading to more reliable predictions of modifier loading on catalysts.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. However, exceptions exist, such as 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, which shows a high yield. This suggests that other structural features or environmental factors may mitigate the sulfanyl group's negative effects.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. However, exceptions exist, such as 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, which shows a high yield. This suggests that other structural features or environmental factors may mitigate the sulfanyl group's negative effects.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

**End of Rules**

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. However, exceptions exist, such as 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, which shows a high yield. This suggests that other structural features or environmental factors may mitigate the sulfanyl group's negative effects.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.6571. However, this is a decrease from the previous high of 0.6857, suggesting that the rules may not be fully optimized yet.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy across iterations suggests that the model is not overfitting. However, the relatively low accuracy indicates potential underfitting, where the rules may not be capturing all the necessary features to predict the loading accurately.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values (e.g., Rule 1 and Rule 4) may be overestimating the loading. Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values (e.g., Rule 6 and Rule 8) may be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The slight decrease in accuracy from 0.6857 to 0.6571 suggests that the current rules may not be capturing all the necessary interactions or features. This indicates room for improvement in the rules to better generalize across different molecules.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights specific rules that contribute significantly to the predictions. For instance, Rule 1 and Rule 4 often have high positive SHAP values, indicating their strong influence on high loading predictions. Adjusting these rules could help in reducing overestimation.
   - Similarly, rules like Rule 6 and Rule 8, which have high negative SHAP values, may need refinement to prevent underestimation of loading.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 1:** Consider adding conditions to account for steric hindrance or electronic effects that may reduce the effectiveness of the carboxylic acid and amino group interaction.
   - **Rule 4:** Introduce additional criteria to account for the influence of substituents on the aromatic ring that may mitigate the formyl group's electron-withdrawing effects.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 6:** Enhance the rule by considering the position and type of electron-donating groups on the pyridine ring, as these can significantly affect coordination with metal centers.
   - **Rule 8:** Expand the rule to include other heterocyclic structures that may provide unique coordination environments, potentially improving yield predictions.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more nuanced rules that consider the interplay between different functional groups and their spatial arrangement. This could help in capturing complex interactions that are currently missed.
   - Consider incorporating additional features or descriptors that capture molecular properties not currently addressed by the rules, such as steric factors or electronic distribution.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**

   - Continuously evaluate the impact of rule changes on validation accuracy to ensure improvements are being made.

By focusing on these areas, the rules can be refined to improve the model's accuracy and generalization, ultimately leading to better predictions of modifier loading on catalysts.

Final Metric Message:

    Target Name: yield
    GPT Model: gpt-4o-2024-08-06
    ML Model: ETC
    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. However, exceptions exist, such as 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, which shows a high yield. This suggests that other structural features or environmental factors may mitigate the sulfanyl group's negative effects.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a nitro group (NO2) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups tend to result in a low yield if the electron-withdrawing effects dominate. An example is 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group can enhance the electrophilic character of the aromatic ring, improving interactions with the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a tertiary alcohol group in a branched aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance and reduced coordination ability. An example is 2-[[1,3-dihydroxy-2-(hydroxymethyl)propan-2-yl]oxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with an aromatic ring containing a carboxylic acid group and an additional electron-withdrawing group, such as a trifluoromethyl group, tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature can reduce effective interaction with the catalyst. An example is 4-[(2,2,2-trifluoroacetyl)amino]butanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) and an amino group tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 8-aminooctanoic acid.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (COOH) and an amino group (NH2) in close proximity, particularly in a non-aromatic context with a carbon chain length of 3 to 5 atoms, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration enhances coordination with metal centers. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid group that also contain electron-donating groups such as amino (NH2) or hydroxy (OH) groups tend to result in a high yield, unless there are additional electron-withdrawing groups present. These groups increase electron density, enhancing interaction with the catalyst. Examples include 4-aminobenzoic acid and 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfanyl group (SH) in a non-aromatic context tend to result in a low yield. However, exceptions exist, such as 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, which shows a high yield. This suggests that other structural features or environmental factors may mitigate the sulfanyl group's negative effects.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a formyl group (CHO) attached to an aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield, unless the aromatic system is heavily substituted with electron-donating groups that can mitigate the electron-withdrawing effects. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with fluorinated alkyl chains tend to result in a low yield. The presence of fluorine alters the electronic properties of the modifier, reducing its effectiveness. An example is 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers containing a pyridine ring with electron-donating substituents, particularly at the 3 or 4 positions, tend to result in a high yield. The nitrogen atom in pyridine can coordinate with metal centers, and electron-donating groups enhance this interaction. Examples include pyridine-3-carboxylic acid and 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic system's ability to stabilize interactions with the catalyst. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with heterocyclic structures other than pyridine or indole, such as pyrazole, tend to result in a high yield. These structures can provide unique coordination environments for metal centers. An example is 1H-pyrazole-4-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a hydroxy group (OH) on a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbon atoms) tend to result in a low yield. The increased flexibility and steric hindrance of long chains may reduce effective coordination with the catalyst. An example is 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages, especially in long chains, tend to result in a low yield. This is likely due to steric hindrance and poor coordination with the metal center. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

The validation accuracy improvement indicates that the model might be underfitting less than before.

3. **Improve Generalization:**

This could involve exploring new chemical properties or interactions not currently covered by the rules.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
